000305scls_0-3STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_d7ba7fe4-7b1d-a50a-a119-70322990c748" anchor="scls_0-3" obligation="informative">
<title id="_2d5d4848-9c06-75db-a29b-fb801bcb76f7">Additions in CityGML 2.0</title>
<p id="_9ab00b06-84dd-61d4-5c00-9a6397fc5747">CityGML 2.0 is a major revision of the previous version 1.0 of this International Standard (OGC Doc. No. 08-007r1), and introduces substantial additions and new features to the thematic model of CityGML. The revision was originally planned to be a minor update to version 1.1. The main endeavor of the revision process was to ensure backwards compatibility both on the level of the conceptual model and on the level of CityGML instance documents. However, some changes could not be implemented consistent with directives for minor revisions and backwards compatibility as enforced by OGC policy (cf. OGC Doc. No. 135r11). The major version number change to 2.0 is therefore a consequence of conforming to the OGC versioning policy without having to abandon any changes or additions which reflect requests from the CityGML community.</p>

<p id="_2069e828-70d0-0112-d15e-9b7f73c6e493">CityGML 2.0 is backwards compatible with version 1.0 in the following sense: each valid 1.0 instance is a valid 2.0 instance provided that the CityGML namespaces and schema locations in the document are changed to their actual 2.0 values. This step is required because the CityGML version number is encoded in these values, but no further actions have to be taken. Hence, there is a simple migration path from existing CityGML 1.0 instances to valid 2.0 instances.</p>
2

001036cls_7STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_005c585b-e83c-64bc-b2df-68ce57503e35" anchor="cls_7" obligation="normative">
<title id="_56b8949b-7204-bca4-1e21-56bd7a47af47">Modularisation</title>
<p id="_47c08eba-7e8e-a024-2bf5-ecf1fa682dbf">CityGML is a rich standard both on the thematic and geometric-topological level of its data model. On its thematic level CityGML defines classes and relations for the most relevant topographic objects in cities and regional models comprising built structures, elevation, vegetation, water bodies, city furniture, and more. In addition to geometry and appearance content these thematic components allow to employ virtual 3D city models for sophisticated analysis tasks in different application domains like simulations, urban data mining, facility management, and thematic inquiries.</p>

<p id="_4313c752-9796-ed24-4ae1-8e4b1db26cb0">CityGML is to be seen as a framework giving geospatial 3D data enough space to grow in geometrical, topological and semantic aspects over its lifetime. Thus, geometry and semantics of city objects may be flexibly structured covering purely geometric datasets up to complex geometric-topologically sound and spatio-semantically coherent data. By this means, CityGML defines a single object model and data exchange format applicable to consecutive process steps of 3D city modelling from geometry acquisition, data qualification and refinement to preparation of data for specific end-user applications, allowing for iterative data enrichment and lossless information exchange (cf. Kolbe et al. 2009).</p>
2



001339cls_8STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_02a81df3-7ee4-d0f0-5d38-7cd847ee967c" anchor="cls_8" obligation="normative">
<title id="_379ad04c-58ed-d60c-c024-cdabc40aa1e3">Spatial model</title>
<p id="_3da91cde-6b66-0765-9e11-f77b75ed941d">Spatial properties of CityGML features are represented by objects of GML3’s geometry model. This model is based on the standard ISO 19107 ‘Spatial Schema’ (Herring 2001), representing 3D geometry according to the well-known  <em>Boundary Representation</em> (B-Rep, cf. Foley et al. 1995). CityGML actually uses only a subset of the GML3 geometry package, defining a profile of GML3. This subset is depicted in  <xref target="fig_9"/> and <xref target="fig_10"/>. Furthermore, GML3’s explicit Boundary Representation is extended by scene graph concepts, which allow the representation of the geometry of features with the same shape implicitly and thus more space efficiently (<xref target="scls_8-2"/>).</p>

<clause id="_e1b3cae1-9805-16a8-873f-b6b185d85f7f" anchor="scls_8-1" obligation="normative">
2



001419scls_8-3STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_79979f38-6d58-67e3-e879-89c747f5baa2" anchor="scls_8-3" obligation="normative">
<title id="_3063f5ee-e8aa-6bdb-4a9f-c5782ed530d4">Implicit geometries, prototypic objects, scene graph concepts</title>
<p id="_09a21cd2-a0d7-679c-0973-242c315c1153">The concept of implicit geometries is an enhancement of the geometry model of GML3. It is, for example, used in CityGML’s building, bridge, tunnel, and vegetation model as well as for city furniture and generic objects. Implicit geometries may be applied to features from different thematic fields of CityGML in order to geometrically represent the features within a specific level of detail (LOD). Thus, each extension module may define spatial properties providing implicit geometries for its thematic classes. For this reason, the concept of implicit geometries is defined within the CityGML core module (cf. <xref target="scls_10-1"/>). However, its description is drawn here since implicit geometries are part of CityGML’s spatial model. The UML diagram is depicted in  <xref target="fig_13"/>. The corresponding XML schema definition is provided in  <xref target="a_A-1"/>.</p>

<p id="_621f10eb-c391-8ae9-5599-9654e674a472">An implicit geometry is a geometric object, where the shape is stored only once as a prototypical geometry, for example a tree or other vegetation objects, a traffic light or a traffic sign. This prototypic geometry object is re-used or referenced many times, wherever the corresponding feature occurs in the 3D city model. Each occurrence is represented by a link to the prototypic shape geometry (in a local cartesian coordinate system), by a transformation matrix that is multiplied with each 3D coordinate of the prototype, and by an anchor point denoting the base point of the object in the world coordinate reference system. This reference point also defines the CRS to which the world coordinates belong after the application of the transformation. In order to determine the absolute coordinates of an implicit geometry, the anchor point coordinates have to be added to the matrix multiplication results. The transformation matrix accounts for the intended rotation, scaling, and local translation of the prototype. It is a 4×4 matrix that is multiplied with the prototype coordinates using homogeneous coordinates, i.e. (x,y,z,1). This way even a projection might be modelled by the transformation matrix.</p>
2



001620cls_9STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_ef352ae5-328f-e301-a97a-b2b266b9d141" anchor="cls_9" obligation="normative">
<title id="_00402411-8ade-c4e8-dea0-a012b0f6bdab">Appearance model</title>
<p id="_31a8c384-7038-c6e3-56fc-22bef0ea621d">In addition to spatial properties, CityGML features have <em>appearances</em> — observable properties of the feature’s surface. Appearances are not limited to visual data but represent arbitrary categories called  <em>themes</em> such as infrared radiation, noise pollution, or earthquake-induced structural stress. Each LOD can have an individual appearance for a specific theme. An appearance is composed of data for each surface geometry object, i.e.  <em>surface data</em>. A single surface geometry object may have surface data for multiple themes. Similarly, surface data can be shared by multiple surface geometry objects (e.g. road paving). Finally, surface data values can either be constant across a surface or depend on the exact location within the surface.</p>

<p id="_654b7bb8-d6a2-1955-1646-de57577f117b">CityGML’s appearance model is defined within the extension module <em>Appearance</em> (cf.  <xref target="cls_7"/>). The UML diagram of the appearance model is illustrated in <xref target="fig_14"/>, for the XML Schema definition see <xref target="a_A-2"/>.</p>

003423scls_9-8STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_40af0af4-9611-e2ca-27eb-db46649e6e20" anchor="scls_9-8" obligation="normative">
<title id="_b39ef3d5-507c-5d15-e7b1-9e40af3653ff">Material model of previous CityGML versions [deprecated]</title>
<p id="_8fba1194-2ec7-dd82-d80b-254c0d216caa">Since GML3 has no built-in concept for the representation of surface materials, previous versions of CityGML extend the GML3 geometry model by the class <em>TexturedSurface</em>, which allows for assigning appearance properties (colors, shininess, transparency) and textures to 3D surfaces. The definition of the appearance properties is adopted from the X3D specification. This approach for appearance modelling has been  <em>deprecated</em> due to inherent limitations. However, in order to provide a certain degree of backwards compatibility for already existing CityGML implementations, the approach has been incorporated into CityGML version 1.0 and version 2.0 as a separate extension module called <em>TexturedSurface</em>. By this means, implementations may employ the old material model by supporting this module. Please note, that appearance information modelled according to the  <em>TexturedSurface</em> module can be converted without information loss to the concepts provided by CityGML’s  <em>Appearance</em> module that has been introduced in the previous clauses of this chapter. Thus, the use of the  <em>TexturedSurface</em> module is <em>strongly discouraged</em> and implementations should only stick to the  <em>Appearance</em> module instead. Moreover, the <em>TexturedSurface</em> module is expected to be removed in future versions of CityGML.</p>

<p id="_c7d1d569-7d12-f29a-baea-cbfdb90bd87b">For the <em>TexturedSurface</em> module, each surface or composite surface can be specialized to a  <em>TexturedSurface</em>, which can be assigned <em>Materials</em> (<em>colors, shininess, transparency</em>) or <em>SimpleTextures</em>. <xref target="fig_20"/> depicts the UML diagram, for XML schema definition see  <xref target="a_A-14"/>.</p>
2



003538cls_10STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_02137374-9986-ce05-9f40-d80a378f187e" anchor="cls_10" obligation="normative">
<title id="_e1366d01-60ce-619e-2370-7430ca4b097d">Thematic model</title>
<p id="_da4a4c05-55a0-8d20-c119-0bf78042c480">The thematic model of CityGML consists of the class definitions for the most important types of objects within virtual 3D city models. These classes have been identified to be either required or important in many different application areas. Most thematic classes are (transitively) derived from the basic classes _<em>Feature</em> and _<em>FeatureCollection</em>, the basic notions defined in ISO 19109 and GML3 for the representation of spatial objects and their aggregations. Features contain spatial as well as non-spatial attributes which are mapped to GML3 feature properties with corresponding data types. Geometric properties are represented as associations to the geometry classes described in <xref target="cls_8"/>. The thematic model also comprises different types of interrelationships between feature classes like aggregations, generalisations and associations.</p>

<p id="_31da6e81-a0e7-d612-8b61-1c383754a51f">The aim of the explicit modelling is to reach a high degree of semantic interoperability between different applications. By specifying the thematic concepts and their semantics along with their mapping to UML and GML3 different applications can rely on a well-defined set of  <em>feature types</em>, attributes and data types with a standardised meaning or interpretation. In order to also allow for the exchange of objects and/or attributes that are not explicitly modelled in CityGML, the concepts of  <em>generic city objects</em> and <em>attributes</em> as well as CityGML’s  <em>Application Domain Extension</em> mechanism have been introduced (cf. <xref target="scls_10-12"/> and <xref target="scls_10-13"/>).</p>
2



003548scls_10-1STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_4f92cec1-e7bd-2a42-a714-ed00f0996f31" anchor="scls_10-1" obligation="normative">
<title id="_f4111890-81e9-6129-f80e-cd924b991306">CityGML Core</title>
<p id="_18cf8b9f-0be9-b532-a645-8bb63c5ba556">The <em>CityGML Core</em> module defines the basic concepts and components of the overall CityGML data model. It forms the universal lower bound of the CityGML data model and, thus, is a dependency of all extension modules. Consequently, the core module has to be implemented by any conformant system. Primarily, the core module provides the abstract base classes from which thematic classes within extension modules are (transitively) derived. Besides abstract type definitions, the core also contains non-abstract content, for example basic data types and thematic classes that may be used by more than one extension module. The UML diagram in  <xref target="fig_21"/> illustrates CityGML’s core module, for the XML Schema definition see below and  <xref target="a_A-1"/>.</p>

<figure id="_10e2bbd3-7614-a748-2404-d31798c7c789" anchor="fig_21" height="346" width="756">
2



003833scls_10-2STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_16850168-404c-19a4-d75d-25f4c9dbe87c" anchor="scls_10-2" obligation="normative">
<title id="_176e39c5-6676-a6c2-66c8-7a6b95606f35">Digital Terrain Model (DTM)</title>
<p id="_509b6981-aa5b-f259-854a-6552812e73e7">An essential part of a city model is the terrain. The Digital Terrain Model (DTM) of CityGML is provided by the thematic extension module  <em>Relief</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>). In CityGML, the terrain is represented by the class <em>ReliefFeature</em> in LOD 0-4 (<xref target="fig_24"/> depicts the UML diagram, for the XML schema definition see  <xref target="a_A-9"/>). A <em>ReliefFeature</em> consists of one or more entities of the class <em>ReliefComponent</em>. Its validity may be restricted to a certain area defined by an optional  <em>validity extent polygon</em>. As <em>ReliefFeature</em> and <em>ReliefComponent</em> are derivatives of _<em>CityObject</em>, the corresponding attributes and relations are inherited. The class  <em>ReliefFeature</em> is associated with different concepts of terrain representations which can coexist. The terrain may be specified as a regular raster or grid (<em>RasterRelief)</em>, as a TIN (Triangulated Irregular Network,  <em>TINReflief</em>), by break lines (<em>BreaklineRelief)</em>, or by mass points  <em>(MasspointRelief)</em>. The four types are implemented by the corresponding GML3 classes: grids by  <em>gml:<em>RectifiedGridCoverage,</em> </em> break lines by <em>gml:MultiCurve,</em> mass points by <em>gml:MultiPoint</em> and TINs either by <em>gml:TriangulatedSurface</em> or by <em>gml:Tin</em>. In case of <em>gml:</em>, the triangles are given explicitly while in case of <em>gml:Tin</em> only 3D points are represented, where the triangulation can be reconstructed by standard methods (Delaunay triangulation, cf. Okabe et al. 1992). Break lines are represented by 3D curves. Mass points are simply a set of 3D points.</p>

<figure id="_b9d2aece-81e8-0437-db79-44301a0f8b53" anchor="fig_24" height="454" width="755">
2

<title id="_ec9c5063-13fa-726d-b15a-a0b11459edfa">Building model</title>
<p id="_1ca1b33b-50ef-56d4-18b3-2b51d718f946">The building model is one of the most detailed thematic concepts of CityGML. It allows for the representation of thematic and spatial aspects of buildings and building parts in five levels of detail, LOD0 to LOD4. The building model of CityGML is defined by the thematic extension module  <em>Building</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>). <xref target="fig_26"/> provides examples of 3D city and building models in LOD1 — 4.</p>

<title id="_f57f8dc1-8870-b302-18bc-4b3476bb7f52">Conformance requirements</title>
<p id="_f17cf17f-ff4f-7d1e-b413-959d1cd2bb56"> <strong>Base requirements</strong> </p>

<ol id="_f8605263-70dc-c107-a4e8-929df64ff0ac" type="arabic"> <li> <p id="_19a10101-a9c1-a989-40eb-e199a50fbe13">If a building only consists of one (homogeneous) part, it shall be represented by the element <em>Building</em>. However, if a building is composed of individual structural segments, it shall be modelled as a <em>Building</em> element having one or more additional <em>BuildingPart</em> elements. Only the geometry and non-spatial properties of the main part of the building should be represented within the aggregating <em>Building</em> element.</p>

006502scls_10-6STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_19aa8a00-3e5b-4e9a-77e1-931a816ae324" anchor="scls_10-6" obligation="normative">
<title id="_bec3d9c8-2d7b-6356-589a-71f35c14b85b">Water bodies</title>
<p id="_86c42c13-5344-c3be-947f-904372f2fdee">Waters have always played an important role in urbanisation processes and cities were built preferably at rivers and places where landfall seemed to be easy. Obviously, water is essential for human alimentation and sanitation. Water bodies present the most economical way of transportation and are barriers at the same time, that avoid instant access to other locations. Bridging waterways caused the first efforts of construction and resulted in high-tech bridges of today. The landscapes of many cities are dominated by water, which directly relates to 3D city models. Furthermore, water bodies are important for urban life as subject of recreation and possible hazards as e.g. floods.</p>

<p id="_bc5ceb0a-9ab0-680f-94f4-f33684e1ccbf">The distinct character of water bodies compared with the permanence of buildings, roadways, and terrain is considered in this thematic model. Water bodies are dynamic surfaces. Tides occur regularly, but irregular events predominate with respect to natural forces, for example flood events. The visible water surface changes in height and its covered area with the necessity to model its semantics and geometry distinct from adjacent objects like terrain or buildings.</p>
2



006717scls_10-7STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_df893298-5545-37b8-4047-d56dc7b0037b" anchor="scls_10-7" obligation="normative">
<title id="_4e07c7b6-017c-a56e-e0a1-05569ca97e10">Transportation objects</title>
<p id="_5859c1f7-c091-4275-bbdb-93934d8c198a">The transportation model of CityGML is a multi-functional, multi-scale model focusing on thematic and functional as well as on geometrical/topological aspects. Transportation features are represented as a linear network in LOD0. Starting from LOD1, all transportation features are geometrically described by 3D surfaces. The areal modelling of transportation features allows for the application of geometric route planning algorithms. This can be useful to determine restrictions and manoeuvres required along a transportation route. This information can also be employed for trajectory planning of mobile robots in the real world or the automatic placement of avatars (virtual people) or vehicle models in 3D visualisations and training simulators. The transportation model of CityGML is provided by the thematic extension module  <em>Transportation</em> (cf.  <xref target="cls_7"/>).</p>

<p id="_357b1c75-d570-e5d0-88c4-b826e0af92a7">The main class is <em>TransportationComplex</em>, which represents, for example, a road, a track, a railway, or a square.  <xref target="fig_57"/> illustrates the four different thematic classes.</p>
2

007026scls_10-8STANDOC_48

<title id="_5df9e034-9883-da68-3956-093e26f3f807">Vegetation objects</title>
<p id="_7df4fe34-3517-a7e1-d031-af17f2e49536">Vegetation features are important components of a 3D city model, since they support the recognition of the surrounding environment. By the analysis and visualisation of vegetation objects, statements on their distribution, structure and diversification can be made. Habitats can be analysed and impacts on the fauna can be derived. The vegetation model may be used as a basis for simulations of, for example forest fire, urban aeration or micro climate. The model could be used, for example to examine forest damage, to detect obstacles (e.g. concerning air traffic) or to perform analysis tasks in the field of environmental protection. The vegetation model of CityGML is defined by the thematic extension module  <em>Vegetation</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>).</p>

<p id="_3ec7b7f0-5d03-c345-43ed-52ded384e385">The vegetation model of CityGML distinguishes between solitary vegetation objects like trees and vegetation areas, which represent biotopes like forests or other plant communities (<xref target="fig_63"/>). Single vegetation objects are modelled by the class  <em>SolitaryVegetationObject</em>, whereas for areas filled with a specific vegetation the class  <em>PlantCover</em> is used. The geometry representation of a  <em>PlantCover</em> feature may be a <em>MultiSurface</em> or a <em>MultiSolid</em>, depending on the vertical extent of the vegetation. For example regarding forests, a <em>MultiSolid</em> representation might be more appropriate. The UML diagram of the vegetation model is depicted in  <xref target="fig_64"/>, for the XML schema definition see below and  <xref target="a_A-12"/>.</p>
2



007244scls_10-9STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_578e6629-6346-fa07-ecca-0c884d4a56e3" anchor="scls_10-9" obligation="normative">
<title id="_a3d7f92d-15af-3e75-fc4a-fa81a62b8133">City furniture</title>
<p id="_d353120d-9034-ea04-9e1c-fcd7b150ec75">City furniture objects are immovable objects like lanterns, traffic lights, traffic signs, flower buckets, advertising columns, benches, delimitation stakes, or bus stops (<xref target="fig_67"/>, <xref target="fig_68"/>). City furniture objects can be found in traffic areas, residential areas, on squares or in built-up areas. The modelling of city furniture objects is used for visualisation of, for example city traffic, but also for analysing local structural conditions. The recognition of special locations in a city model is improved by the use of these detailed city furniture objects, and the city model itself becomes more alive and animated. The city furniture model of CityGML is defined by the thematic extension module  <em>CityFurniture</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>).</p>

<p id="_aa44b0a4-a53a-fc48-0e27-16238ee673ba">City furniture objects can have an important influence on simulations of, for example city traffic situations. Navigation systems can be realised, for example for visually handicapped people using a traffic light as routing target. Likewise, city furniture objects are important to plan a heavy vehicle transportation, where the exact position and further conditions of obstacles must be known.</p>
2



007439scls_10-10STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_091af0b9-48b6-73b9-bb82-cbc6c85074c8" anchor="scls_10-10" obligation="normative">
<title id="_958752fc-5949-5152-cc8d-ea84131f8a2e">Land use</title>
<p id="_5bb56df5-1578-644a-2f99-01084da7d16e"> <em>LandUse</em> objects can be used to describe areas of the earth’s surface dedicated to a specific land use, but also to describe areas of the earth’s surface having a specific land cover with or without vegetation, such as sand, rock, mud flats, forest, grasslands, etc (i.e. the physical appearance). Land use and land cover are different concepts; the first describes human activities on the earth’s surface, the second describes its physical and biological cover. However, the two are interlinked and often mixed in practice.  <em>LandUse</em> objects in CityGML represent both concepts: They can be employed to represent, parcels, spatial planning objects, recreational objects and objects describing the physical characteristics of an area, in 3D (e.g. wetlands).  <xref target="fig_71"/> shows the UML diagram of land use objects, for the XML schema definition see  <xref target="scls_10-10-1"/> and  <xref target="a_A-8"/>. The land use model of CityGML is provided by the thematic extension module  <em>LandUse</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>).</p>

<figure id="_a77d40b8-81d3-2c59-3834-0b882591dd08" anchor="fig_71" height="223" width="613">

007502scls_10-11STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_439b79bf-103e-9523-998f-d2092d674e63" anchor="scls_10-11" obligation="normative">
<title id="_1597dded-41ee-929a-c6a9-a803f60b900a">City object groups</title>
<p id="_6ef38a2c-21cf-f5be-9a3b-79ce96ee0162">The CityGML grouping concept has been introduced in <xref target="scls_6-8"/>. <em>CityObjectGroups</em> are modelled using the Composite Design Patternfrom software engineering (cf. Gamma et al. 1995):  <em>CityObjectGroups</em> aggregate <em>CityObjects</em> and furthermore are defined as special  <em>CityObjects</em>. This implies that a group may become a member of another group realizing a recursive aggregation schema. However, in a CityGML instance document it has to be ensured (by the generating application) that no cyclic groupings are included.  <xref target="fig_73"/> shows the UML diagram for the class  <em>CityObjectGroup,</em> forthe XML schema see <xref target="a_A-6"/>. The grouping concept of CityGML is defined by the thematic extension module <em>CityObjectGroup</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>).</p>

<figure id="_c981d882-787a-645b-0c55-85c5b4ab5564" anchor="fig_73" height="256" width="445">

007580scls_10-12STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_6bf1aa05-c83f-f455-7e49-3b3c14d6e090" anchor="scls_10-12" obligation="normative">
<title id="_fa26695f-0995-bccf-633d-7862f472375c">Generic city objects and attributes</title>
<p id="_7d769118-4986-b278-5dea-558ec6cb83a6">The concept of generic city objects and attributes allows for the storage and exchange of 3D objects which are not covered by any explicitly modelled thematic class within CityGML or which require attributes not represented in CityGML. These generic extensions to the CityGML data model are realised by the classes <em>GenericCityObject</em> and _<em>genericAttribute</em> defined within the thematic extension module  <em>Generics</em> (cf. <xref target="cls_7"/>). In order to avoid problems concerning semantic interoperability, generic extensions shall only be used if appropriate thematic classes or attributes are not provided by any other CityGML module.</p>

<p id="_2b3e726c-4588-ae17-fec6-6af566800e99"> <xref target="fig_74"/> shows the UML diagram of generic objects and attributes. For XML schema definition see below and <xref target="a_A-7"/>.</p>

007789scls_10-13STANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<clause id="_17998c25-04df-c9f4-8887-a2e0a9489dfa" anchor="scls_10-13" obligation="normative">
<title id="_f72b93a5-5650-0c43-b186-e0b7e6260dcf">Application Domain Extensions (ADE)</title>
<p id="_813861ac-79e8-bce6-26bd-3693fe502fe0">CityGML has been designed as an application independent information model and exchange format for 3D city and landscape models. However, specific applications typically have additional information needs to be modelled and exchanged. In general, there are two different approaches to combine city model data and application data:</p>

<ol id="_a19c0ebb-fabb-8250-a75d-6ff861fe420c" type="arabic"> <li> <p id="_8ca587f2-a829-7be1-9456-c1fdfeb84330">Embed the CityGML objects into a (larger) application framework and establish the connection between application data and CityGML data within the application framework. For example, CityGML data fragments may be embedded into the application’s XML data files or stored as attributes of application objects according to the application’s data model.</p>
2

011525_c28ee09b-7610-5a36-5d1f-fa0cb61755adSTANDOC_48
(generic warning) Table should have title
<table id="_c28ee09b-7610-5a36-5d1f-fa0cb61755ad" unnumbered="true"> <tbody> <tr id="_5205a2fa-d8ee-2ff0-707e-1899bf445a0e"> <td id="_c084b4ec-32e8-c593-4a6b-26067b5b3cde" valign="middle" align="left"> <strong>a) Test purpose</strong> </td>
<td id="_272da541-584f-4236-c0af-b38195b381fd" valign="middle" align="left">Verify that a profile employed by a CityGML instance document is a valid CityGML profile in accordance with the rules and guidelines stated in <xref target="scls_7-2"/>. For CityGML profile definitions provided by a separate XML Schema definition file (referenced as second approach in <xref target="scls_7-2"/>), verify that the profile’s XML Schema definition file itself is valid and imports all schema definitions of CityGML modules that are used to represent the data within the instance document and, thus, are part of the employed CityGML profile. The target namespace of the profile’s XML Schema definition must differ from the namespaces of the imported CityGML modules and must be given as previously unused and globally unique URI. The profile’s XML Schema definition must not contain any further content.</td>
</tr> <tr id="_9fe51fc1-b28b-e0d9-d145-dc5c710c5305"> <td id="_c720fa75-ba92-76e9-fcfb-06580523ebf3" valign="middle" align="left"> <strong>b) Test method</strong> </td>
<td id="_43bd5b3c-7218-3400-d7cb-e02e6b4c5f10" valign="middle" align="left">Validate the XML Schema definition of the CityGML profile. The process may be using an appropriate software tool for validation or be a manual process that checks all relevant definitions from the respective XML Schema specification of the CityGML profile. Inspect the instance document and check that it satifies the rules for employing CityGML profiles described in <xref target="scls_7-2"/> (second approach).</td>
</tr> <tr id="_78a84ab3-de3e-8f8c-4a3f-e046159830b8"> <td id="_54e14ca3-e8c2-d21b-3103-76b5a40d6fe6" valign="middle" align="left"> <strong>c) Reference</strong> </td>

012031annex_cSTANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<annex id="_965ae677-6e11-ae3c-e588-f53414225fc7" anchor="annex_c" obligation="informative">
<title id="_7dd6be83-0354-d90a-a18e-10fd6fc4a686">Code lists proposed by the SIG 3D</title>
<p id="_d749c7d9-0177-947c-45d2-1a3e6eca06f8">This annex exemplifies the specification of code lists for enumerative attributes of type <em>gml:CodeType</em> and provides proposals for selected attributes. Please note that this annex is non-normative and the presented code lists are neither mandatory nor complete. The governance of code lists is explicitly decoupled from the governance of the CityGML schema and specification (cf. <xref target="scls_10-14"/>). Thus, code lists can be freely defined outside the CityGML specification by any organization or information community according to their information needs, and there shall be one authority per code list who is in charge of maintance and updating the contents.</p>

<p id="_6758b0b9-0642-ab1d-873c-6d794d297900">The code lists presented in this annex are managed and maintained by the SIG 3D and are accessible at <link target="http://www.sig3d.de/codelists/standard/">http://www.sig3d.de/codelists/standard/</link>. They completely comprise the contents of the non-normative code lists given in the previous CityGML 1.0 specification document (cf. <xref target="annex_c"/> of OGC Doc. No. 08-007r1). In order to maintain backwards compatibility, none of the previous entries has been modified or changed in meaning. Additional code lists have been added for some of the newly introduced enumerative attributes in CityGML 2.0, for example in the context of the <em>Bridge</em> and <em>Tunnel</em> module. The SIG 3D code lists will be complemented subsequently. The previous version 1.0 code lists are still accessible from the OGC repository at <link target="http://schemas.opengis.net/citygml/codelists/">http://schemas.opengis.net/citygml/codelists/</link>.</p>
2

019864annex_gSTANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<annex id="_20c6a60d-8f86-5cdd-1372-6f04c53f9148" anchor="annex_g" obligation="informative">
<title id="_479bec6e-89e9-0564-73a4-daf04db54d80">Example CityGML datasets</title>
<p id="_61ac81fe-b20a-bfdc-3ac1-e26d12de1eb1">The following chapters provide example CityGML models and excerpts of the corresponding CityGML instance documents. In  <xref target="a_G-1"/> to <xref target="a_G-6"/>, a single CityGML building is subsequently illustrated and refined from a coarse LOD0 representation up to a semantically rich and geometric-topologically sound LOD4 model including the building interior. The model exemplifies concepts from the CityGML  <em>Building</em> module and demonstrates the coherent modelling of semantics and geometric/topological properties. In  <xref target="a_G-7"/>, the building and its surrounding terrain are enriched with visual appearance information to show the usage of the CityGML  <em>Appearance</em> module. A further example for the <em>Appearance</em> module is presented in  <xref target="a_G-8"/> illustrating the texturing of polygons with inner holes. Finally, the use of a local engineering coordinate reference system in CityGML is illustrated in  <xref target="a_G-9"/>.</p>

<p id="_2291b03e-6902-13f8-1a3f-c6fbc401eafb">All CityGML instance documents contained in this annex are bundled with the CityGML schema package, and are available at <link target="http://schemas.opengis.net/citygml/examples/2.0/">http://schemas.opengis.net/citygml/examples/2.0/</link>.</p>
2



021876annex_hSTANDOC_48
(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause
<annex id="_d4774fee-f6be-6f3b-93f6-f331dd01bbb6" anchor="annex_h" obligation="informative">
<title id="_f2d3cf74-66bd-eca2-cb7c-dae604f29f2a">Example ADE for Noise Immission Simulation</title>
<p id="_1e818047-b449-6391-9480-e27f0e006628">This annex illustrates the usage of CityGML within an environmental simulation application. The definition of the corresponding Application Domain Extension (ADE) is included as an example.</p>

<p id="_665ac4f6-7c48-0a97-aad3-84051e2f5890">The Environmental Noise Directive of the European Union 2002/49/EG obligates the EU member states to calculate every 5 years the noise levels at a height of 4m on buildings and to document the results in noise maps. The noise maps serve as information for the European Union and the citizens affected by noise (<xref target="fig_94"/>). These noise maps are generated on the basis of acoustic models and noise propagation calculations, not on the basis of measurements (<xref target="fig_95"/>). For the noise propagation calculations, a great number of thematic data and 3D geodata is necessary for each EU member state. Because of the large spatial extent of the noise calculation, the provision of statewide and ubiquitous 3D geodata on buildings, roads, railways and terrain for a multitude of users is necessary, in part with high requirements on resolution.</p>
2

(generic warning) Hanging paragraph in clause

<title id="_e162f95c-71ad-08c9-76f4-edecc6b25ecd">Example ADE for Ubiquitous Network Robots Services</title>
<p id="_3d6472ed-8f58-0a52-c065-4b83218980e3">This annex illustrates the usage of CityGML within Robotics services. The definition of the corresponding  <em>Application Domain Extension</em> (ADE) is included as an example.</p>
